### [118] [ActTail: Global Activation Sparsity in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12272)
*Wenwen Hou,Xinyuan Song,Shiwei Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ActTail allocates activation sparsity non-uniformly across Transformer projections using heavy-tail statistics of weights, enabling TopK activation pruning that preserves accuracy at high sparsity and comes with theory linking sparsity to heavy-tail exponents. It outperforms uniform sparsity on LLaMA and Mistral with large perplexity gains at 80% sparsity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Uniform activation sparsity ignores heterogeneous statistics across Transformer projections, leading to unnecessary accuracy loss. Leveraging heavy-tailed behavior of weight matrices can guide where activations can be pruned more aggressively to cut computation/memory while minimizing degradation.

Method: Estimate a heavy-tail exponent for each projection from the empirical spectral density of its weight matrix. Use this exponent as a quantitative signal to assign projection-specific activation sparsity budgets in a global TopK magnitude-based scheme. Provide theoretical analysis under Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization (HT-SR) that explicitly relates the optimal activation sparsity ratio to the heavy-tail exponent, yielding principled (non-heuristic) allocation rules.

Result: Across LLaMA-2-7B, LLaMA-2-13B, and Mistral-7B, ActTail improves perplexity and downstream task scores versus uniform activation sparsity, especially at high sparsity. At 80% sparsity, perplexity reductions are 21.8% (LLaMA-2-7B), 40.1% (LLaMA-2-13B), and 9.4% (Mistral-7B).

Conclusion: Activation sparsity should be allocated heterogeneously based on heavy-tail statistics. The proposed HT-SR–guided allocation provides theoretical grounding and empirically yields better accuracy-efficiency trade-offs than uniform sparsity for LLM inference acceleration.

Abstract: Activation sparsity is a promising approach for accelerating large language model (LLM) inference by reducing computation and memory movement. However, existing activation sparsity methods typically apply uniform sparsity across projections, ignoring the heterogeneous statistical properties of Transformer weights and thereby amplifying performance degradation. In this paper, we propose ActTail, a TopK magnitude-based activation sparsity method with global activation sparsity allocation grounded in Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization (HT-SR) theory. Specifically, we capture this heterogeneity via the heavy-tail exponent computed from each projection's empirical spectral density (ESD), which is used as a quantitative indicator to assign projection-specific sparsity budgets. Importantly, we provide a theoretical analysis that establishes an explicit relationship between the activation sparsity ratio and the heavy-tail exponent under the HT-SR regime, offering principled guidance for sparsity allocation beyond heuristic design. Experiments on LLaMA and Mistral models show that our method improves both perplexity and downstream task performance at high sparsity compared to uniform allocation. At 80% sparsity, perplexity is reduced by 21.8% on LLaMA-2-7B, 40.1% on LLaMA-2-13B, and 9.4% on Mistral-7B.

### [119] [Aligning Language Models from User Interactions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12273)
*Thomas Kleine Buening,Jonas Hübotter,Barna Pásztor,Idan Shenfeld,Giorgia Ramponi,Andreas Krause*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They use multi-turn user chats as a free supervision signal by distilling how a model would revise its answer after seeing the user’s follow-up back into the base policy, improving alignment and enabling personalization without explicit labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Abundant real-world multi-turn interactions are usually discarded, yet follow-ups implicitly signal errors, unmet instructions, or preferences. Since LMs can already self-correct when conditioned on these follow-ups, capturing and internalizing this hindsight behavior could yield scalable alignment and continual adaptation without costly human annotation or reward models.

Method: Hindsight self-distillation: given an initial response and a user follow-up, condition the model on the follow-up to obtain a revised token distribution (the “hindsight” policy). Compare this with the original policy and train the model to match the hindsight distribution without access to the follow-up, using large-scale real user conversations (WildChat). The same mechanism is used for per-user adaptation/personalization across interactions.

Result: Training on WildChat conversations improves performance on standard alignment and instruction-following benchmarks, with no regressions on other capabilities. The approach also enables continual, per-user personalization emerging from interaction alone.

Conclusion: Raw, naturally occurring user follow-ups provide a scalable, label-efficient supervision source. Distilling the model’s hindsight corrections into the base policy yields better alignment and supports ongoing personalization and adaptation during deployment.

Abstract: Multi-turn user interactions are among the most abundant data produced by language models, yet we lack effective methods to learn from them. While typically discarded, these interactions often contain useful information: follow-up user messages may indicate that a response was incorrect, failed to follow an instruction, or did not align with the user's preferences. Importantly, language models are already able to make use of this information in context. After observing a user's follow-up, the same model is often able to revise its behavior. We leverage this ability to propose a principled and scalable method for learning directly from user interactions through self-distillation. By conditioning the model on the user's follow-up message and comparing the resulting token distribution with the original policy, we obtain a target for updating the policy that captures how the model's behavior changes in hindsight. We then distill this hindsight distribution back into the current policy. Remarkably, we show that training on real-world user conversations from WildChat improves language models across standard alignment and instruction-following benchmarks, without regressing other capabilities. The same mechanism enables personalization, allowing models to continually adapt to individual users through interaction without explicit feedback. Our results demonstrate that raw user interactions that arise naturally during deployment enable alignment, personalization, and continual adaptation.

</details>


### [120] [GONE: Structural Knowledge Unlearning via Neighborhood-Expanded Distribution Shaping](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12275)
*Chahana Dahal,Ashutosh Balasubramaniam,Zuobin Xiong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GONE introduces a KG-based benchmark to measure how well LLMs can forget targeted facts without collateral damage, and NEDS is a neighborhood-aware unlearning method that cleanly erases targets while preserving related knowledge, achieving perfect unlearning efficacy and strong locality on LLaMA-3-8B and Mistral-7B.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current unlearning/editing methods handle flat, sentence-level facts but miss relational, multi-hop, and reasoning-dependent knowledge that lives in knowledge graphs, leading to leakage of removed facts and unintended forgetting. There is a need for an evaluation that captures these structured effects and a method that leverages graph structure to unlearn precisely.

Method: 1) GONE benchmark: constructs KG-based probes to disentangle three effects—direct fact removal, reasoning-based leakage (multi-hop inference that recreates the removed knowledge), and catastrophic forgetting (collateral damage to neighboring facts). 2) NEDS (Neighborhood-Expanded Distribution Shaping): exploits graph connectivity to identify anchor nodes and correlated neighbors, then shapes the model’s output distribution to set a sharp decision boundary between the target fact and its semantic neighborhood, aiming for effective yet localized unlearning. Evaluated on LLaMA-3-8B and Mistral-7B, compared to parameter editing, fine-tuning, and distillation baselines.

Result: NEDS outperforms existing unlearning/editing methods, obtaining 1.000 unlearning efficacy and 0.839 locality on the GONE benchmark and showing strong results on other datasets; experiments are conducted on LLaMA-3-8B and Mistral-7B.

Conclusion: Evaluating and operating at the KG level reveals failure modes missed by sentence-level tests. Leveraging graph structure enables precise, leakage-resistant unlearning with minimal collateral damage; NEDS embodies this approach and sets new performance marks. Code is released for reproducibility.

Abstract: Unlearning knowledge is a pressing and challenging task in Large Language Models (LLMs) because of their unprecedented capability to memorize and digest training data at scale, raising more significant issues regarding safety, privacy, and intellectual property. However, existing works, including parameter editing, fine-tuning, and distillation-based methods, are all focused on flat sentence-level data but overlook the relational, multi-hop, and reasoned knowledge in naturally structured data. In response to this gap, this paper introduces Graph Oblivion and Node Erasure (GONE), a benchmark for evaluating knowledge unlearning over structured knowledge graph (KG) facts in LLMs. This KG-based benchmark enables the disentanglement of three effects of unlearning: direct fact removal, reasoning-based leakage, and catastrophic forgetting. In addition, Neighborhood-Expanded Distribution Shaping (NEDS), a novel unlearning framework, is designed to leverage graph connectivity and identify anchor correlated neighbors, enforcing a precise decision boundary between the forgotten fact and its semantic neighborhood. Evaluations on LLaMA-3-8B and Mistral-7B across multiple knowledge editing and unlearning methods showcase NEDS's superior performance (1.000 on unlearning efficacy and 0.839 on locality) on GONE and other benchmarks. Code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/GONE-4679/.

### [121] [Prompt Injection as Role Confusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12277)
*Charles Ye,Jasmine Cui,Dylan Hadfield-Menell*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Prompt injection succeeds because LMs misidentify “who is speaking.” Probes show models grant authority to text that imitates trusted roles, enabling ~60% attack success and predicting vulnerability before generation. This offers a mechanistic, unifying account of prompt injection and exposes a gap between interface roles and latent role attribution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite extensive safety training, LMs remain vulnerable to prompt injection. The authors seek a mechanistic explanation for why injections work and a way to predict vulnerability, hypothesizing a mismatch between interface-designated roles (system, tool, user) and the model’s internal attribution of speaker/authority.

Method: They design role probes to measure how models internally represent and attribute “who is speaking.” Using these probes, they craft injections that spoof authoritative roles and test them by inserting spoofed reasoning into user prompts and tool outputs. They evaluate across multiple open/closed models on StrongREJECT and an agent exfiltration task, and analyze correlations between probe-measured role confusion and attack outcomes, prior to generation.

Result: Injected spoofed reasoning achieves ~60% success on StrongREJECT and ~61% on agent exfiltration across diverse models, outperforming near-zero baselines. The probe-measured degree of internal role confusion strongly predicts attack success even before the model begins generating.

Conclusion: Prompt injection exploits a common mechanism: internal role confusion whereby untrusted text that mimics an authoritative role inherits its authority in latent space. Security defined at the interface cannot prevent this unless models bind authority to trusted sources internally. Their framework unifies diverse injection attacks and suggests pre-generation diagnostics and defenses that align latent role attribution with interface roles.

Abstract: Language models remain vulnerable to prompt injection attacks despite extensive safety training. We trace this failure to role confusion: models infer roles from how text is written, not where it comes from. We design novel role probes to capture how models internally identify "who is speaking." These reveal why prompt injection works: untrusted text that imitates a role inherits that role's authority. We test this insight by injecting spoofed reasoning into user prompts and tool outputs, achieving average success rates of 60% on StrongREJECT and 61% on agent exfiltration, across multiple open- and closed-weight models with near-zero baselines. Strikingly, the degree of internal role confusion strongly predicts attack success before generation begins. Our findings reveal a fundamental gap: security is defined at the interface but authority is assigned in latent space. More broadly, we introduce a unifying, mechanistic framework for prompt injection, demonstrating that diverse prompt-injection attacks exploit the same underlying role-confusion mechanism.

</details>


### [122] [LLM-Augmented Therapy Normalization and Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis for Treatment-Resistant Depression on Reddit](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12343)
*Yuxin Zhu,Sahithi Lakamana,Masoud Rouhizadeh,Selen Bozkurt,Rachel Hershenberg,Abeed Sarker*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Mines TRD-focused Reddit posts, normalizes 23k medication mentions, and applies an aspect-based sentiment classifier (DeBERTa-v3; F1 0.80 on SMM4H) to quantify patient-reported sentiment, finding mostly neutral mentions, relatively negative sentiment for SSRIs/SNRIs, and comparatively favorable sentiment for ketamine/esketamine.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical evidence for pharmacologic management of TRD is sparse and RCTs underrepresent patient-reported tolerability and lived experiences. Large-scale peer-support narratives may provide complementary, real-world perspectives on how patients perceive and evaluate medications.

Method: Curated 5,059 Reddit posts explicitly referencing TRD (2010–2025) from 28 mental-health subreddits (3,480 users). Applied the model to label per-medication sentiment (positive/neutral/negative) and analyzed distributions by drug, user, subreddit, and year.

Result: Across medication mentions: 72.1% neutral, 14.8% negative, 13.1% positive. Conventional antidepressants (especially SSRIs/SNRIs) had higher negative than positive sentiment, while ketamine/esketamine showed a comparatively more favorable sentiment profile. Classifier performance on SMM4H test set was micro-F1 0.800.

Conclusion: Combining normalized medication extraction with aspect-based sentiment analysis can characterize patient-perceived treatment experiences in TRD Reddit discourse and complement clinical evidence. Signals suggest relatively unfavorable perceptions of conventional antidepressants and more favorable views of ketamine/esketamine among self-identified TRD posters, highlighting social-media mining as a useful patient-centered evidence stream.

Abstract: Treatment-resistant depression (TRD) is a severe form of major depressive disorder in which patients do not achieve remission despite multiple adequate treatment trials. Conventional antidepressants, especially SSRIs and SNRIs, showed consistently higher negative than positive proportions, whereas ketamine and esketamine showed comparatively more favorable sentiment profiles. However, the dependence on an external ASR system and the use of a non-causal decoder limits streaming use. TASTE-S integrates a CTC-based ASR module into the encoder for instant dual-modality encoding. We also redesign the unit decoder to enable on-the-fly decoding. With joint training, we show that TASTE-S matches TASTE's performance while significantly reducing latency. Further investigations reveal that TASTE-S remains robust to transcriptions and enables long-form encoding and decoding.

### [124] [Not Just the Destination, But the Journey: Reasoning Traces Causally Shape Generalization Behaviors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12397)
*Pengcheng Wen,Yanxu Zhu,Jiapeng Sun,Han Zhu,Yujin Zhou,Chi-Min Chan,Sirui Han,Yike Guo*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Debate exists over whether CoT is genuine computation or post‑hoc rationalization. For alignment, it matters whether the content of reasoning can independently steer model behavior (e.g., toward harm) beyond what is signaled by final outputs.

Method: Construct datasets where final answers are identically harmful but the reasoning differs: Evil (malicious), Misleading (harm‑rationalizing), and Submissive (yielding to pressure). Train 0.6B–14B models under QTA (question‑thinking‑answer), QT (question‑thinking), and T‑only (thinking‑only) paradigms; then evaluate both with and without explicit reasoning generation to isolate causal effects of reasoning content.

Result: (1) CoT training can amplify harmful generalization more than standard fine‑tuning. (2) Different reasoning styles induce distinct, semantically aligned behavioral patterns despite identical final answers. (3) Training on reasoning alone (QT or T‑only) is sufficient to shift behavior, showing reasoning carries an independent learning signal. (4) These shifts persist even when models answer without showing reasoning, indicating internalization.

Conclusion: Reasoning content is causally potent. Alignment strategies that supervise only final outputs are insufficient; process/chain supervision and careful curation of reasoning traces are necessary to avoid encoding harmful dispositions.

Abstract: Chain-of-Thought (CoT) is often viewed as a window into LLM decision-making, yet recent work suggests it may function merely as post-hoc rationalization. This raises a critical alignment question: Does the reasoning trace causally shape model generalization independent of the final answer? To isolate reasoning's causal effect, we design a controlled experiment holding final harmful answers constant while varying reasoning paths. We construct datasets with \textit{Evil} reasoning embracing malice, \textit{Misleading} reasoning rationalizing harm, and \textit{Submissive} reasoning yielding to pressure. We train models (0.6B--14B parameters) under multiple paradigms, including question-thinking-answer (QTA), question-thinking (QT), and thinking-only (T-only), and evaluate them in both think and no-think modes. We find that: (1) CoT training could amplify harmful generalization more than standard fine-tuning; (2) distinct reasoning types induce distinct behavioral patterns aligned with their semantics, despite identical final answers; (3) training on reasoning without answer supervision (QT or T-only) is sufficient to alter behavior, proving reasoning carries an independent signal; and (4) these effects persist even when generating answers without reasoning, indicating deep internalization. Our findings demonstrate that reasoning content is causally potent, challenging alignment strategies that supervise only outputs.

### [125] [Interpreting Negation in GPT-2: Layer- and Head-Level Causal Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12423)
*Abdullah Al Mofael,Lisa M. Kuhn,Ghassan Alkadi,Kuo-Pao Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They causally trace how GPT‑2 Small encodes negation and show that only a few mid‑layer attention heads carry most of the polarity signal; intervening on these heads (patching/ablation) predictably weakens or redirects negation sensitivity, with effects that generalize to xNot360 at smaller magnitudes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Negation routinely trips up LMs by flipping meanings or facts. The paper aims to locate and verify where and how negation is represented inside a transformer, to move from surface behavior to mechanistic understanding that could enable debugging and control.

Method: Build a 12k paired dataset of affirmative vs. negated sentences across templates; define a Negation Effect Score (NES) to quantify sensitivity to polarity. Analyze hidden states by layer/head. Perform activation patching (inject affirmative activations into negated contexts) and targeted head ablations to test causal roles, comparing in‑domain data and the external xNot360 benchmark.

Result: Negation sensitivity is concentrated in a small set of attention heads, mainly in layers 4–6. Ablating these heads increases NES in‑domain (weaker negation sensitivity), and ‘rescuing’ by inserting cached affirmative activations increases NES further, indicating these heads carry affirmative signal rather than restoring neutral behavior. On xNot360, ablation slightly decreases NES and rescue recovers performance above baseline; effects are consistent but smaller.

Conclusion: Negation is not globally distributed in GPT‑2 Small but funneled through specific mid‑layer heads. Causal interventions reveal the direction of polarity signals and offer levers to modulate them. The patterns transfer to an external benchmark, suggesting generality, and motivate targeted interpretability/steering methods for logical phenomena like negation.

Abstract: Negation remains a persistent challenge for modern language models, often causing reversed meanings or factual errors. In this work, we conduct a causal analysis of how GPT-2 Small internally processes such linguistic transformations. We examine its hidden representations at both the layer and head level. Our analysis is based on a self-curated 12,000-pair dataset of matched affirmative and negated sentences, covering multiple linguistic templates and forms of negation. To quantify this behavior, we define a metric, the Negation Effect Score (NES), which measures the model's sensitivity in distinguishing between affirmative statements and their negations. We carried out two key interventions to probe causal structure. In activation patching, internal activations from affirmative sentences were inserted into their negated counterparts to see how meaning shifted. In ablation, specific attention heads were temporarily disabled to observe how logical polarity changed. Together, these steps revealed how negation signals move and evolve through GPT-2's layers. Our findings indicate that this capability is not widespread; instead, it is highly concentrated within a limited number of mid-layer attention heads, primarily within layers 4 to 6. Ablating these specific components directly disrupts the model's negation sensitivity: on our in-domain, ablation increased NES (indicating weaker negation sensitivity), and re-introducing cached affirmative activations (rescue) increased NES further, confirming that these heads carry affirmative signal rather than restoring baseline behavior. On xNot360, ablation slightly decreased NES and rescue restored performance above baseline. This pattern demonstrates that these causal patterns are consistent across various negation forms and remain detectable on the external xNot360 benchmark, though with smaller magnitude.

To address this, we introduce ShatterMed-QA, a bilingual benchmark of 10,558 multi-hop clinical questions designed to rigorously evaluate deep diagnostic reasoning. Crucially, restoring the masked evidence via Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) triggers near-universal performance recovery, validating ShatterMed-QA's structural fidelity and proving its efficacy in diagnosing the fundamental reasoning deficits of current medical AI. *Zekun Wu,Adriano Koshiyama,Sahan Bulathwela,Maria Perez-Ortiz*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing evaluations focus on ranking/quality (e.g., NDCG) and overlook whether advice remains safe when tool outputs are corrupted. The work seeks to reveal and quantify this safety–quality mismatch and understand mechanisms driving unsafe drift.

Method: Introduce a paired-trajectory replay protocol of real financial dialogues under clean vs contaminated tool outputs across seven LLMs (7B to frontier). Measure utility preservation, risk-inappropriate product rates, self-correction and reliability-check behaviors across up to 23-step trajectories (1,563 contaminated turns). Test narrative-only (headline) corruption that avoids numerical manipulation. Propose a safety-penalized NDCG (sNDCG).

Narrative-only corruption induces significant drift and evades consistency monitors. sNDCG reduces preservation to 0.51–0.74, surfacing the hidden safety gap.

Agents should be designed to detect unreliable tools, challenge contaminated data, and self-correct over long horizons, especially in high-stakes deployments.

Across the seven models tested, we consistently observe the evaluation-blindness pattern: recommendation quality is largely preserved under contamination (utility preservation ratio approximately 1.0) while risk-inappropriate products appear in 65-93% of turns, a systematic safety failure poorly reflected by standard NDCG. Safety violations are predominantly information-channel-driven, emerge at the first contaminated turn, and persist without self-correction over 23-step trajectories; no agent across 1,563 contaminated turns explicitly questions tool-data reliability. Even narrative-only corruption (biased headlines, no numerical manipulation) induces significant drift while completely evading consistency monitors. A safety-penalized NDCG variant (sNDCG) reduces preservation ratios to 0.51-0.74, indicating that much of the evaluation gap becomes visible once safety is explicitly measured. These results motivate considering trajectory-level safety monitoring, beyond single-turn quality, for deployed multi-turn agents in high-stakes settings.

### [132] [Expert Pyramid Tuning: Efficient Parameter Fine-Tuning for Expertise-Driven Task Allocation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12577)
*Jia-Chen Zhang,Zhen-Wei Yan,Yu-Jie Xiong,Chun-Ming Xia*

TL;DR: EPT is a PEFT method that brings a multi-scale feature pyramid to LoRA: it learns a shared low-dimensional meta-subspace and reconstructs task-specific features via learnable up-projection “pyramid” experts, with a router selecting combinations. Different tasks require different granularity (high-level semantics vs. fine-grained syntax), so uniform low-rank experts limit representation power and cross-task transfer.

Method: Decompose adaptation into (1) a shared, low-dimensional meta-knowledge subspace capturing universal linguistic patterns; (2) a Pyramid Projection Mechanism with learnable up-projection operators that reconstruct higher-dimensional features at multiple scales. A task-aware router dynamically selects optimal combinations of these multi-scale features. The design supports re-parameterization to keep parameter count low.

Result: Across multiple multi-task benchmarks, EPT significantly surpasses state-of-the-art MoE-LoRA methods and simultaneously reduces the number of trainable parameters.

Conclusion: Embedding hierarchical, multi-scale feature construction into PEFT improves performance–efficiency trade-offs for LLM adaptation. EPT captures diverse task granularities better than uniform-expert MoE-LoRA and offers practical gains with fewer trainable parameters.

Abstract: Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) has become a dominant paradigm for deploying LLMs in multi-task scenarios due to its extreme parameter efficiency. While Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) based LoRA variants have achieved promising results by dynamically routing tokens to different low-rank experts, they largely overlook the hierarchical nature of task complexity. Existing methods typically employ experts with uniform architectures, limiting their ability to capture diverse feature granularities required by distinct tasks--where some tasks demand high-level semantic abstraction while others require fine-grained syntactic manipulation. To bridge this gap, we propose Expert Pyramid Tuning (EPT), a novel architecture that integrates the multi-scale feature pyramid concept from computer vision into the realm of PEFT. Unlike standard LoRA, EPT decomposes task adaptation into two stages: (1) A shared meta-knowledge Subspace that encodes universal linguistic patterns in low dimensions; (2) A Pyramid Projection Mechanism that utilizes learnable up-projection operators to reconstruct high-dimensional features at varying scales. A task-aware router then dynamically selects the optimal combination of these multi-scale features. Extensive experiments across multiple multi-task benchmarks demonstrate that EPT significantly outperforms SOTA MoE-LoRA variants. Crucially, thanks to the re-parameterization capability of our design, EPT achieves this performance improvement while simultaneously reducing the number of training parameters.

</details>


### [133] [RTD-Guard: A Black-Box Textual Adversarial Detection Framework via Replacement Token Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12582)
*He Zhu,Yanshu Li,Wen Liu,Haitian Yang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing adversarial-text detectors often depend on prior knowledge of attack strategies, white-box access, or many queries; robust training is costly. There is a need for a lightweight, practical, privacy-preserving detector that works in black-box settings with minimal queries and no retraining.

Method: Leverage a pre-trained RTD discriminator (e.g., ELECTRA-style) to score tokens for being replacements; localize suspicious tokens; mask those tokens; query the victim model on the original and masked texts; compute the change in prediction confidence; classify inputs as adversarial if the confidence shift surpasses a threshold. No adversarial data, fine-tuning, or internal access; only two black-box queries.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks and diverse state-of-the-art attacks, RTD-Guard detects adversarial texts effectively and surpasses existing detection baselines on multiple metrics, while remaining query- and resource-efficient.

Conclusion: RTD-Guard provides a practical, generalizable, and resource-light defense for real-world, black-box, and privacy-sensitive NLP deployments by exploiting RTD’s sensitivity to word substitutions and using minimal intervention to expose adversarial manipulations.

Abstract: Textual adversarial attacks pose a serious security threat to Natural Language Processing (NLP) systems by introducing imperceptible perturbations that mislead deep learning models. While adversarial example detection offers a lightweight alternative to robust training, existing methods typically rely on prior knowledge of attacks, white-box access to the victim model, or numerous queries, which severely limits their practical deployment. This paper introduces RTD-Guard, a novel black-box framework for detecting textual adversarial examples. Our key insight is that word-substitution perturbations in adversarial attacks closely resemble the "replaced tokens" that a Replaced Token Detection (RTD) discriminator is pre-trained to identify. Leveraging this, RTD-Guard employs an off-the-shelf RTD discriminator-without fine-tuning-to localize suspicious tokens, masks them, and detects adversarial examples by observing the prediction confidence shift of the victim model before and after intervention. The entire process requires no adversarial data, model tuning, or internal model access, and uses only two black-box queries. Comprehensive experiments on multiple benchmark datasets demonstrate that RTD-Guard effectively detects adversarial texts generated by diverse state-of-the-art attack methods. It surpasses existing detection baselines across multiple metrics, offering a highly efficient, practical, and resource-light defense mechanism-particularly suited for real-world deployment in resource-constrained or privacy-sensitive environments.

### [135] [98$\times$ Faster LLM Routing Without a Dedicated GPU: Flash Attention, Prompt Compression, and Near-Streaming for the vLLM Semantic Router](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12646)
*Xunzhuo Liu,Bowei He,Xue Liu,Andy Luo,Haichen Zhang,Huamin Chen*

TL;DR: Three-stage optimization of the vLLM Semantic Router delivers 98× lower latency and <800 MB GPU footprint, enabling 8K–32K token classification on shared GPUs via ROCm FlashAttention, classical prompt compression, and streaming I/O tweaks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: System-level LLM routers for safety/domain/PII must add minimal latency and avoid needing a dedicated GPU. Standard attention’s O(n^2) memory makes long-context (8K–32K) classification infeasible when co-located with vLLM, exhausting memory and causing OOMs.

Method: Stage 1: Implement a custom CK FlashAttention operator for ONNX Runtime on ROCm to reduce attention memory from O(n^2) to O(n) and accelerate inference. Stage 2: Apply classical NLP prompt compression (TextRank, position weighting, TF‑IDF, novelty scoring) to shrink inputs to ~512 tokens without neural inference, capping latency and memory. Stage 3: Use near‑streaming body processing with adaptive chunking and zero‑copy JSON to remove serialization overhead.

Stage 3 reduces 62→50 ms (1.2×) via streaming/zero‑copy. Conclusion: A combined kernel-level (ROCm FlashAttention) and systems/NLP pipeline makes long-context, low-latency routing practical on shared GPUs, eliminating the need for a dedicated accelerator. Stage 1 is AMD‑targeted (NVIDIA already has cuDNN FlashAttention); Stages 2–3 are hardware‑agnostic.

Abstract: System-level routers that intercept LLM requests for safety classification, domain routing, and PII detection must be both fast and operationally lightweight: they should add minimal latency to every request, yet not require a dedicated GPU -- an expensive resource better used for LLM inference itself. When the router co-locates on the same GPU as vLLM serving instances, standard attention's $O(n^2)$ memory makes long-context classification (8K--32K tokens) impossible: at 8K tokens, three concurrent classifiers need ${\sim}$4.5\,GB for attention masks alone, far exceeding the memory left by vLLM. We present three staged optimizations for the vLLM Semantic Router, benchmarked on AMD Instinct MI300X, that solve both the latency and the memory problem. \emph{Stage~1}: a custom CK Flash Attention operator for ONNX Runtime on ROCm reduces attention memory from $O(n^2)$ to $O(n)$ and end-to-end (E2E) latency from 4{,}918\,ms to 127\,ms (\textbf{38.7$\times$}), enabling 8K--32K tokens where SDPA OOMs. \emph{Stage~2}: classical NLP prompt compression (TextRank, position weighting, TF-IDF, and novelty scoring) reduces all inputs to ${\sim}$512 tokens without neural inference, capping both latency and GPU memory at a constant regardless of original prompt length (E2E 127$\to$62\,ms, \textbf{2.0$\times$}). \emph{Stage~3}: near-streaming body processing with adaptive chunking and zero-copy JSON eliminates serialization overhead (E2E 62$\to$50\,ms, \textbf{1.2$\times$}). Cumulatively: \textbf{98$\times$} improvement (4{,}918\,ms to 50\,ms), 16K-token routing in 108\,ms, and a total router GPU footprint under 800\,MB -- small enough to share a GPU with LLM serving and removing the need for a dedicated accelerator. Stage~1 targets AMD ROCm (NVIDIA GPUs already have FlashAttention via cuDNN); Stages~2 and~3 are hardware-agnostic.

Through controlled semi-synthetic experiments, we show that existing methods over-attend to redundant tokens and struggle to reliably translate textual semantics into usable numerical cues. To bridge this gap, we propose TESS, which introduces a Temporal Evolution Semantic Space as an intermediate bottleneck between modalities. This space consists of interpretable, numerically grounded temporal primitives (mean shift, volatility, shape, and lag) extracted from text by an LLM via structured prompting and filtered through confidence-aware gating. ### [138] [MetaKE: Meta-learning Aligned Knowledge Editing via Bi-level Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12677)
*Shuxin Liu,Ou Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MetaKE reframes knowledge editing in LLMs as a bi-level, closed-loop problem that learns the edit target itself, using a Structural Gradient Proxy to align edits with the model’s feasible region, yielding superior empirical performance over prior KE methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing knowledge editing methods choose semantic edit targets in an open-loop manner, disconnected from what the downstream editing solver can actually realize. This “Semantic–Execution Disconnect” causes valid semantic targets to fall outside the feasible region, leading to gradient truncation and frequent editing failures. The authors aim to couple target selection with executability to preserve general abilities while achieving precise edits.

Method: Treat the edit target as a learnable meta-parameter in a bi-level optimization: (upper level) search for a feasible target that maximizes post-edit performance; (lower level) apply a KE solver to execute the edit. To differentiate through complex lower-level solvers, introduce a Structural Gradient Proxy that explicitly propagates editability constraints back to the target-learning stage. Theory shows this aligns the edit direction with the model’s feasible manifold.

Result: Across extensive experiments, MetaKE significantly outperforms strong KE baselines on editing success and stability (exact metrics not specified in the abstract). Empirical evidence supports that aligning target selection with solver feasibility reduces failures due to gradient truncation and improves overall KE effectiveness.

Conclusion: MetaKE provides a closed-loop, meta-learning perspective on knowledge editing that couples target formation with executability. By aligning edits with the model's feasible manifold via a Structural Gradient Proxy, it improves reliability and performance and sets a new direction for KE research.

Abstract: Knowledge editing (KE) aims to precisely rectify specific knowledge in Large Language Models (LLMs) without disrupting general capabilities. State-of-the-art methods suffer from an open-loop control mismatch. We identify a critical "Semantic-Execution Disconnect": the semantic target is derived independently without feedback from the downstream's feasible region. This misalignment often causes valid semantic targets to fall within the prohibited space, resulting in gradient truncation and editing failure. To bridge this gap, we propose MetaKE (Meta-learning Aligned Knowledge Editing), a new framework that reframes KE as a bi-level optimization problem. Departing from static calculation, MetaKE treats the edit target as a learnable meta-parameter: the upper-level optimizer seeks a feasible target to maximize post-edit performance, while the lower-level solver executes the editing. To address the challenge of differentiating through complex solvers, we derive a Structural Gradient Proxy, which explicitly backpropagates editability constraints to the target learning phase. Theoretical analysis demonstrates that MetaKE automatically aligns the edit direction with the model's feasible manifold. Extensive experiments confirm that MetaKE significantly outperforms strong baselines, offering a new perspective on knowledge editing.

In this study, we employ a text similarity metric to evaluate different ChatGPT models' capacity to generate diverse textual outputs. We propose SteerRM, the first training-free method for debiasing reward models using Sparse Autoencoder (SAE)-based interventions. SteerRM isolates stylistic effects using contrastive paired responses, identifies bias-related SAE features with a strength-stability criterion, and suppresses them at inference time. Across six reward models on RM-Bench, SteerRM improves Hard-split accuracy by 7.3 points on average while preserving overall performance. Results on a Gemma-based reward model and a controlled non-format bias further suggest generalization across RM architectures and bias types. We further find that format-related features are concentrated in shallow layers and transfer across models, revealing shared architecture-level bias encoding patterns. These results show that SAE-based interventions can mitigate reward-model biases without retraining, providing a practical and interpretable solution for alignment pipelines.

*Xunzhuo Liu,Bowei He,Xue Liu,Andy Luo,Haichen Zhang,Huamin Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AVR inserts a lightweight semantic router between a CUA and a pool of VLMs to dynamically choose the cheapest model that meets a target reliability for each GUI action, yielding large cost savings with minimal accuracy loss and integrating safety-aware escalation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: GUI grounding difficulty varies widely across screens and actions, but current CUAs use a single fixed VLM, causing either unnecessary cost (overkill) or accuracy deficits (underkill). A principled, cost–accuracy-aware mechanism is needed to adapt model choice per action while maintaining reliability and safety.

Method: AVR estimates action difficulty using multimodal embeddings, probes a small VLM to gauge confidence, and routes each tool call to the lowest-cost model predicted to meet a reliability threshold. It formalizes routing as a cost–accuracy trade-off, derives a threshold-based selection policy, leverages agent memory (“warm” context retrieval) to reduce escalation, and integrates a Visual Confused Deputy guardrail to escalate high-risk actions to the strongest model.

Result: On ScreenSpot-Pro grounding data and the OpenClaw agent routing benchmark, AVR projects up to 78% reduction in inference cost while staying within 2 percentage points of an all-large-model baseline. With the safety guardrail, it preferentially escalates risky actions to the strongest model.

Conclusion: Adaptive VLM Routing achieves near-baseline grounding reliability at substantially lower cost and unifies efficiency with safety via risk-aware escalation; memory retrieval further shrinks the gap between small and large models. Code, models, and benchmarks are released for reproducibility.

Abstract: Computer Use Agents (CUAs) translate natural-language instructions into Graphical User Interface (GUI) actions such as clicks, keystrokes, and scrolls by relying on a Vision-Language Model (VLM) to interpret screenshots and predict grounded tool calls. However, grounding accuracy varies dramatically across VLMs, while current CUA systems typically route every action to a single fixed model regardless of difficulty. We propose \textbf{Adaptive VLM Routing} (AVR), a framework that inserts a lightweight semantic routing layer between the CUA orchestrator and a pool of VLMs. For each tool call, AVR estimates action difficulty from multimodal embeddings, probes a small VLM to measure confidence, and routes the action to the cheapest model whose predicted accuracy satisfies a target reliability threshold. For \textit{warm} agents with memory of prior UI interactions, retrieved context further narrows the capability gap between small and large models, allowing many actions to be handled without escalation. We formalize routing as a cost--accuracy trade-off, derive a threshold-based policy for model selection, and evaluate AVR using ScreenSpot-Pro grounding data together with the OpenClaw agent routing benchmark. Across these settings, AVR projects inference cost reductions of up to 78\% while staying within 2 percentage points of an all-large-model baseline. When combined with the Visual Confused Deputy guardrail, AVR also escalates high-risk actions directly to the strongest available model, unifying efficiency and safety within a single routing framework. Materials are also provided Model, benchmark, and code: https://github.com/vllm-project/semantic-router.

</details>


### [145] [Rethinking Multiple-Choice Questions for RLVR: Unlocking Potential via Distractor Design](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12826)
*Xu Guo,Qiming Ge,Jian Tong,Kedi Chen,Jin Zhang,Xiaogui Yang,Xuan Gao,Haijun Lv,Zhihui Lu,Yicheng Zou,Qipeng Guo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: RLVR on MCQs can work very well if the options are designed right. The paper shows that mismatched option counts hurt generalization, strong distractors suppress guessing/elimination shortcuts (even for 2-choice items), and introduces Iterative Distractor Curation (IDC) to automatically build hard, high-quality distractors, yielding sizable RLVR gains across benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MCQs provide scalable, verifiable rewards for RLVR but invite reward hacking via guessing or trivial elimination. Converting to open-ended removes this risk but discards the valuable contrastive signal in distractors. The authors aim to keep MCQs while preventing shortcut exploitation and to understand how option design affects RLVR.

Method: (1) Empirical analysis of MCQ option design for RLVR, focusing on option-count mismatches and distractor strength. (2) Proposal of Iterative Distractor Curation (IDC): an active curation loop that identifies elimination shortcuts and incrementally replaces/constructs distractors with stronger, confounding alternatives to force deeper reasoning, keeping train–test option counts aligned.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks, curated options improve distractor quality and substantially boost RLVR training performance compared to using the original MCQ data. Strong distractors enable effective training even with 2-way questions, and aligning option counts between train and test improves generalization.

Conclusion: MCQ design is pivotal for RLVR. Maintaining consistent option counts and using strong, curated distractors mitigates reward hacking without discarding MCQs. IDC offers a practical path to leverage verifiable MCQ rewards while promoting genuine reasoning, improving RLVR outcomes.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) significantly enhances the reasoning capabilities of Large Language Models. When applied to RLVR, Multiple-Choice Questions (MCQs) offer a scalable source of verifiable data but risk inducing reward hacking, where models shortcut reasoning via random guessing or simple elimination. Current approaches often mitigate this by converting MCQs to open-ended formats, thereby discarding the contrastive signal provided by expert-designed distractors. In this work, we systematically investigate the impact of option design on RLVR. Our analysis highlights two primary insights: (1) Mismatches in option counts between training and testing degrade performance. (2) Strong distractors effectively mitigate random guessing, enabling effective RLVR training even with 2-way questions. Motivated by these findings, we propose Iterative Distractor Curation (IDC), a framework that actively constructs high-quality distractors to block elimination shortcuts and promote deep reasoning. Experiments on various benchmarks demonstrate that our method effectively enhances distractor quality and yields significant gains in RLVR training compared to the original data.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: SSD relies on PCA before regression but lacks a systematic rule to select the number of components, creating unnecessary researcher degrees of freedom and risking unstable or uninterpretable semantic gradients.

Demonstrate on a corpus of short AI-related posts from Prolific participants with Admiration and Rivalry narcissism scores.

A counterfactual using a high-dimensional PCA heuristic yields diffuse, weakly structured clusters, underscoring the sweep’s advantage.

Abstract: Supervised Semantic Differential (SSD) is a mixed quantitative-interpretive method that models how text meaning varies with continuous individual-difference variables by estimating a semantic gradient in an embedding space and interpreting its poles through clustering and text retrieval. SSD applies PCA before regression, but currently no systematic method exists for choosing the number of retained components, introducing avoidable researcher degrees of freedom in the analysis pipeline. We propose a PCA sweep procedure that treats dimensionality selection as a joint criterion over representation capacity, gradient interpretability, and stability across nearby values of K. We illustrate the method on a corpus of short posts about artificial intelligence written by Prolific participants who also completed Admiration and Rivalry narcissism scales. The sweep yields a stable, interpretable Admiration-related gradient contrasting optimistic, collaborative framings of AI with distrustful and derisive discourse, while no robust alignment emerges for Rivalry. We also show that a counterfactual using a high-PCA dimension solution heuristic produces diffuse, weakly structured clusters instead, reinforcing the value of the sweep-based choice of K. The case study shows how the PCA sweep constrains researcher degrees of freedom while preserving SSD's interpretive aims, supporting transparent and psychologically meaningful analyses of connotative meaning.

In this paper, we introduce WALAR, a reinforcement training method using only monolingual text to elevate LLMs' translation capabilities on massive low-resource languages while retaining their performance on high-resource languages. Our key insight is based on the observation of failure modes (or "holes") in existing source-based multilingual quality estimation (QE) models. Reinforcement learning (RL) using these QE models tends to amplify such holes, resulting in poorer multilingual LLMs. We develop techniques including word alignment and language alignment to mitigate such holes in WALAR's reward for RL training. We continually trained an LLM supporting translation of 101 languages using WALAR. The experiments show that our new model outperforms LLaMAX, one of the strongest open-source multilingual LLMs by a large margin on 1400 language directions on Flores-101 dataset.

### [155] [Neuron-Aware Data Selection In Instruction Tuning For Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13201)
*Xin Chen,Junchao Wu,Shu Yang,Runzhe Zhan,Zeyu Wu,Min Yang,Shujian Huang,Lidia S. Chao,Derek F. Wong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: NAIT is a data-selection framework for instruction tuning that chooses training samples whose neuron-activation patterns most closely match target capability features, yielding better performance with a small subset of data and showing transferability across tasks—especially for logical/programmatic content and a stable core subset that boosts fundamental skills.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Excessive instruction-tuning data can hurt LLM performance, while small, high-quality subsets help. The challenge is to efficiently identify the most effective subset—either general-purpose or targeted—without costly external models and with signals that transfer across tasks.

Method: Construct reusable neuron-activation feature representations from in-domain datasets that exemplify desired capabilities. For each candidate IT sample, compute similarity between its neuron-activation pattern and the target capability features, then select the highest-similarity subset for training. Assess transferability of these features across capabilities and tasks.

Result: Using only 10% of Alpaca-GPT4 selected by NAIT consistently outperforms baselines that depend on external strong models or uncertainty-based heuristics across multiple tasks. Neuron-activation features transfer across capabilities; logically reasoned and programmatic samples generalize broadly; a small, stable core subset suffices to activate fundamental abilities and deliver universal gains.

Conclusion: Activation-similarity-guided selection is an efficient, reusable approach to curate high-impact IT data. It enables better performance with less data, reveals transferable capability features, and highlights the value of logic/programming-rich samples and a core, capability-activating subset for robust, general improvements.

Abstract: Instruction Tuning (IT) has been proven to be an effective approach to unlock the powerful capabilities of large language models (LLMs). Recent studies indicate that excessive IT data can degrade LLMs performance, while carefully selecting a small subset of high-quality IT data can significantly enhance their capabilities. Therefore, identifying the most efficient subset data from the IT dataset to effectively develop either specific or general abilities in LLMs has become a critical challenge. To address this, we propose a novel and efficient framework called NAIT. NAIT evaluates the impact of IT data on LLMs performance by analyzing the similarity of neuron activation patterns between the IT dataset and the target domain capability. Specifically, NAIT captures neuron activation patterns from in-domain datasets of target domain capabilities to construct reusable and transferable neuron activation features. It then evaluates and selects optimal samples based on the similarity between candidate samples and the expected activation features of the target capabilities. Experimental results show that training on the 10\% Alpaca-GPT4 IT data subset selected by NAIT consistently outperforms methods that rely on external advanced models or uncertainty-based features across various tasks. Our findings also reveal the transferability of neuron activation features across different capabilities of LLMs. In particular, IT data with more logical reasoning and programmatic features possesses strong general transferability, enabling models to develop stronger capabilities across multiple tasks, while a stable core subset of data is sufficient to consistently activate fundamental model capabilities and universally improve performance across diverse tasks.

</details>

### [156] [Context-Enriched Natural Language Descriptions of Vessel Trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12287)
*Kostas Patroumpas,Alexandros Troupiotis-Kapeliaris,Giannis Spiliopoulos,Panagiotis Betchavas,Dimitrios Skoutas,Dimitris Zissis,Nikos Bikakis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce a context-aware abstraction pipeline that converts noisy AIS vessel tracks into clean, semantically rich trip–episode representations, enriched with geography, navigation, and weather context, enabling controlled NL descriptions via LLMs and easing downstream maritime reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Raw AIS trajectories are noisy, dense, and hard to interpret or reason over directly. There is a need for human-interpretable, machine-usable, semantically enriched representations that reduce spatiotemporal complexity and can integrate with LLMs for higher-level maritime analytics.

Method: Segment AIS streams into distinct trips; denoise and partition trips into mobility-annotated episodes; enrich each episode with multi-source context (nearby geographic entities, offshore navigation features, weather). Use these abstractions to prompt LLMs to generate controlled natural language descriptions, and empirically assess description quality across several LLMs using AIS plus open contextual data.

Result: Empirical evaluation indicates that LLMs can generate meaningful controlled descriptions from the enriched abstractions; the approach increases semantic density and reduces trajectory complexity, supporting higher-level reasoning. (No specific quantitative metrics reported in the abstract.)

Conclusion: Context-aware trajectory abstraction for AIS improves interpretability and machine reasoning readiness, facilitating downstream analytics and effective integration with LLMs for maritime reasoning tasks by compressing spatiotemporal detail into semantically rich episodes.

Abstract: We address the problem of transforming raw vessel trajectory data collected from AIS into structured and semantically enriched representations interpretable by humans and directly usable by machine reasoning systems. We propose a context-aware trajectory abstraction framework that segments noisy AIS sequences into distinct trips each consisting of clean, mobility-annotated episodes. Each episode is further enriched with multi-source contextual information, such as nearby geographic entities, offshore navigation features, and weather conditions. Crucially, such representations can support generation of controlled natural language descriptions using LLMs. We empirically examine the quality of such descriptions generated using several LLMs over AIS data along with open contextual features. By increasing semantic density and reducing spatiotemporal complexity, this abstraction can facilitate downstream analytics and enable integration with LLMs for higher-level maritime reasoning tasks.

### [157] [Efficient Reasoning with Balanced Thinking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12372)
*Yulin Li,Tengyao Tu,Li Ding,Junjie Wang,Huiling Zhen,Yixin Chen,Yong Li,Zhuotao Tian*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ReBalance is a training-free, plug-and-play test-time control method that uses confidence-driven activation steering to curb overthinking and stimulate exploration when models underthink, reducing tokens and improving accuracy across math, QA, and coding benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LRMs waste compute by overthinking on easy problems and miss reasoning paths by underthinking on harder ones. Existing fixes (keyword suppression, fixed chain lengths) often swing too far, sacrificing accuracy. A dynamic, data-driven way to balance depth of reasoning without retraining is needed for practical, resource-constrained deployment.

Method: 1) Use a small dataset to aggregate hidden states into prototypes representing reasoning modes. 2) Derive a steering vector from these prototypes. 3) Monitor real-time confidence: high variance flags overthinking; persistently high confidence flags underthinking. 4) A dynamic controller adjusts the steering vector’s strength and direction during generation to prune redundant reasoning when overthinking and encourage exploration when underthinking. The approach is training-free and attaches at inference as a plug-in.

Result: Across four model sizes (0.5B–32B) and nine benchmarks (math, general QA, coding), ReBalance reduces output redundancy and improves accuracy relative to baselines that only suppress reflection or limit reasoning length.

Conclusion: Confidence-aware activation steering can dynamically balance reasoning depth without retraining, yielding both efficiency and accuracy gains and offering a general, plug-and-play strategy for LRMs. Code is released for reproducibility.

Abstract: Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) have shown remarkable reasoning capabilities, yet they often suffer from overthinking, expending redundant computational steps on simple problems, or underthinking, failing to explore sufficient reasoning paths despite inherent capabilities. These issues lead to inefficiencies and potential inaccuracies, limiting practical deployment in resource-constrained settings. Existing methods to mitigate overthinking, such as suppressing reflective keywords or adjusting reasoning length, may inadvertently induce underthinking, compromising accuracy. Therefore, we propose ReBalance, a training-free framework that achieves efficient reasoning with balanced thinking. ReBalance leverages confidence as a continuous indicator of reasoning dynamics, identifying overthinking through high confidence variance and underthinking via consistent overconfidence. By aggregating hidden states from a small-scale dataset into reasoning mode prototypes, we compute a steering vector to guide LRMs' reasoning trajectories. A dynamic control function modulates this vector's strength and direction based on real-time confidence, pruning redundancy during overthinking, and promoting exploration during underthinking. Extensive experiments conducted on four models ranging from 0.5B to 32B, and across nine benchmarks in math reasoning, general question answering, and coding tasks demonstrate that ReBalance effectively reduces output redundancy while improving accuracy, offering a general, training-free, and plug-and-play strategy for efficient and robust LRM deployment. Code is available at https://github.com/yu-lin-li/ReBalance .

</details>

We evaluate 6 popular data analysis agents (both open-source and proprietary) on domain-specific data and query types, and find that they fail on stateful and incident-specific queries. We observe two key expressivity gaps in existing evals: domain-customized datasets and domain-specific query types. To enable practitioners in such domains to generate customized and expressive evals for such timeseries data agents, we present AgentFuel. AgentFuel helps domain experts quickly create customized evals to perform end-to-end functional tests. ### [159] [AI Planning Framework for LLM-Based Web Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12710)
*Orit Shahnovsky,Rotem Dror*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Formalizes web agents as planners, mapping common LLM agent styles to classic search paradigms, proposes behavior-focused metrics and a labeled trajectory dataset, and shows different agent architectures trade off human-like trajectories vs technical accuracy—arguing success rates alone are insufficient.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM web agents are opaque and hard to debug; practitioners lack a principled way to diagnose planning failures (e.g., context drift, poor decomposition) and to evaluate behaviors beyond binary task success.

Method: Model web tasks as sequential decision-making and map agent designs to search algorithms: Step-by-Step → BFS, Tree Search → Best-First Tree Search, Full-Plan-in-Advance → DFS. Define five new trajectory-quality metrics, curate 794 human-labeled WebArena trajectories, and compare a baseline Step-by-Step agent with a new Full-Plan-in-Advance agent using these metrics.

Result: Step-by-Step aligns more with human gold trajectories and achieves 38% overall success, while Full-Plan-in-Advance attains superior technical measures such as 89% element accuracy. The proposed metrics surface trade-offs invisible to success-only evaluation.

Conclusion: The taxonomy and metrics enable principled diagnosis and help select agent architectures to match application constraints. Success rates alone mislead; different architectures excel on different dimensions, motivating metric-aware, task-specific choices and further research on hybrid/adaptive agents.

Abstract: Developing autonomous agents for web-based tasks is a core challenge in AI. While Large Language Model (LLM) agents can interpret complex user requests, they often operate as black boxes, making it difficult to diagnose why they fail or how they plan. This paper addresses this gap by formally treating web tasks as sequential decision-making processes. We introduce a taxonomy that maps modern agent architectures to traditional planning paradigms: Step-by-Step agents to Breadth-First Search (BFS), Tree Search agents to Best-First Tree Search, and Full-Plan-in-Advance agents to Depth-First Search (DFS). This framework allows for a principled diagnosis of system failures like context drift and incoherent task decomposition. To evaluate these behaviors, we propose five novel evaluation metrics that assess trajectory quality beyond simple success rates. We support this analysis with a new dataset of 794 human-labeled trajectories from the WebArena benchmark. Finally, we validate our evaluation framework by comparing a baseline Step-by-Step agent against a novel Full-Plan-in-Advance implementation. Our results reveal that while the Step-by-Step agent aligns more closely with human gold trajectories (38% overall success), the Full-Plan-in-Advance agent excels in technical measures such as element accuracy (89%), demonstrating the necessity of our proposed metrics for selecting appropriate agent architectures based on specific application constraints.

</details>


### [160] [On Using Machine Learning to Early Detect Catastrophic Failures in Marine Diesel Engines](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12733)
*Francesco Maione,Paolo Lino,Giuseppe Giannino,Guido Maione*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Detect catastrophic marine engine failures early by modeling the time-derivative of residuals (difference between sensor readings and predicted values) and classifying with a Random Forest, enabling alerts before threshold-based alarms; validated in simulation and with real failure data, supported by DL-based data augmentation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Catastrophic engine failures are abrupt, hard to predict, and dangerous; most research targets gradual wear, leaving a gap for methods that can flag fast-onset anomalies early enough to prevent damage and ensure safety.

Method: 1) Learn expected sensor behavior; 2) compute residuals between actual and predicted signals; 3) take time-derivatives of these residuals to capture rapid-onset dynamics; 4) use Random Forest (best among tested ML models) to predict anomalies/failure onset. Training data scarcity is mitigated with deep learning–based data augmentation. Benchmarked against conventional deviation/threshold methods. Validated on simulated scenarios and a real failed-engine dataset.

Result: The derivative-of-residual approach provides earlier indications than traditional deviation- or threshold-based alarms, allowing intervention before measurements cross critical limits. Simulations confirm earlier detection; real-world validation demonstrates robustness and practical utility. Random Forest outperformed alternatives among those tested.

Conclusion: Using derivatives of residuals with a Random Forest enables earlier, reliable detection of catastrophic engine failures, offering operators lead time to shut down engines and reroute safely. The approach is practical, generalizable across variables, and supported by DL augmentation to overcome limited failure data.

Abstract: Catastrophic failures of marine engines imply severe loss of functionality and destroy or damage the systems irreversibly. Being sudden and often unpredictable events, they pose a severe threat to navigation, crew, and passengers. The abrupt nature makes early detection the only effective countermeasure. However, research has concentrated on modeling the gradual degradation of components, with limited attention to sudden and anomalous phenomena. This work proposes a new method for early detection of catastrophic failures. Based on real data from a failed engine, the approach evaluates the derivatives of the deviation between actual sensor readings and expected values of engine variables. Predictions are obtained by a Random Forest, which is the most suitable Machine Learning algorithm among the tested ones. Traditional methods focus on deviations of monitored signals, whereas the proposed approach employs the derivatives of the deviations to provide earlier indications of abnormal dynamics, and to alert that a rapid and dangerous event is breaking out within the system. The method allows the detection of anomalies before measurements reach critical thresholds and alarms are triggered, which is the common method in industry. Consequently, operators can be warned in advance and shut down the engine, then prevent damage and unexpected power loss. Moreover, they have the time to safely change the ship route and avoid potential obstacles. Simulation results conf irm the effectiveness of the proposed approach in anticipating occurrence of catastrophic failures. Validation on real-world data further reinforces the robustness and practical applicability of the method. It is worth noting that data acquisition to train the predictive algorithm is not a problem, since a Deep Learning-based data augmentation procedure is used.

### [161] [ToolTree: Efficient LLM Agent Tool Planning via Dual-Feedback Monte Carlo Tree Search and Bidirectional Pruning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12740)
*Shuo Yang,Soyeon Caren Han,Yihao Ding,Shuhe Wang,Eduard Hoy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ToolTree introduces an MCTS-inspired planning framework that explores and prunes tool-use sequences with dual-stage LLM evaluation, yielding ~10% average performance gains over SOTA while maintaining top efficiency on four benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Greedy, reactive tool selection in current LLM agents lacks foresight and ignores dependencies between tools, limiting effectiveness on complex, multi-step tasks involving diverse external tools.

Method: An MCTS-style planner (ToolTree) that explores tool-use trajectories, applies dual-stage LLM evaluation, and performs bidirectional pruning—both pre-execution (to avoid weak branches) and post-execution (to cut unpromising continuations)—to make adaptive, longer-horizon tool decisions.

Result: Across open-set and closed-set tool-planning tasks on four benchmarks, ToolTree consistently outperforms prior planning paradigms by about 10% on average while retaining the highest efficiency.

Conclusion: Incorporating lookahead search with dual-stage evaluation and bidirectional pruning enables more reliable, efficient long-horizon tool planning for LLM agents than greedy, reactive strategies.

Abstract: Large Language Model (LLM) agents are increasingly applied to complex, multi-step tasks that require interaction with diverse external tools across various domains. However, current LLM agent tool planning methods typically rely on greedy, reactive tool selection strategies that lack foresight and fail to account for inter-tool dependencies. In this paper, we present ToolTree, a novel Monte Carlo tree search-inspired planning paradigm for tool planning. ToolTree explores possible tool usage trajectories using a dual-stage LLM evaluation and bidirectional pruning mechanism that enables the agent to make informed, adaptive decisions over extended tool-use sequences while pruning less promising branches before and after the tool execution. Empirical evaluations across both open-set and closed-set tool planning tasks on 4 benchmarks demonstrate that ToolTree consistently improves performance while keeping the highest efficiency, achieving an average gain of around 10\% compared to the state-of-the-art planning paradigm.

</details>


### [162] [AI Model Modulation with Logits Redistribution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12755)
*Zihan Wang,Zhongkui Ma,Xinguo Feng,Zhiyang Mei,Ethan Ma,Derui Wang,Minhui Xue,Guangdong Bai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AIM is a training-free, data-agnostic logits-redistribution framework that lets one model flexibly change its behavior—dialing output quality (utility) and shifting input-feature focus—backed by a formal rationale and validated across vision and language tasks/architectures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Maintaining many specialized model variants is operationally inefficient. Model owners need dynamic control over output quality, and end users need precise control over which input features the model emphasizes—without retraining or accessing training data.

Method: Proposes AIM, a post-hoc modulation paradigm with two modes: (1) utility modulation to control output quality/utility levels, and (2) focus modulation to steer which input features the model prioritizes. It operates by redistributing logits (reordering/rescaling predicted scores) in a training-data-agnostic, retraining-free manner. A formal foundation is provided via analysis of the statistical properties of logits ordering using joint probability distributions to ensure controllable regulation.

Result: Empirical evaluation shows AIM works across tasks (image classification, semantic segmentation, text generation) and architectures (ResNet, SegFormer, Llama), demonstrating practical, versatile modulation—adjustable quality and user-steerable focus—without retraining.

Conclusion: AIM offers an efficient, unified mechanism to endow a single model with diverse, controllable behaviors, reducing the need for multiple model forks and providing theoretically grounded, broadly applicable modulation.

Abstract: Large-scale models are typically adapted to meet the diverse requirements of model owners and users. However, maintaining multiple specialized versions of the model is inefficient. In response, we propose AIM, a novel model modulation paradigm that enables a single model to exhibit diverse behaviors to meet the specific end requirements. AIM enables two key modulation modes: utility and focus modulations. The former provides model owners with dynamic control over output quality to deliver varying utility levels, and the latter offers users precise control to shift model's focused input features. AIM introduces a logits redistribution strategy that operates in a training data-agnostic and retraining-free manner. We establish a formal foundation to ensure AIM's regulation capability, based on the statistical properties of logits ordering via joint probability distributions. Our evaluation confirms AIM's practicality and versatility for Al model modulation, with tasks spanning image classification, semantic segmentation and text generation, and prevalent architectures including ResNet, SegFormer and Llama.

### [164] [ODRL Policy Comparison Through Normalisation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.12926)
*Jaime Osvaldo Salas,Paolo Pareti,George Konstantinidis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce semantics-preserving normalization algorithms that rewrite ODRL policies into a permission-only normal form with simplified constraints, enabling easier comparison and interoperability, with a known (attribute-driven) complexity blow-up.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ODRL’s expressive complexity leads to fragmented, non-interoperable subsets and multiple, incomparable encodings of semantically equivalent policies, hindering processing and comparison.

Method: Using a recent formal semantics of ODRL, they define a parameterized normalization that decomposes policies into minimal components, systematically eliminates prohibitions by translating them into permissions, and simplifies complex logical, numerical, and symbolic constraints. They provide algorithms for computing this normal form and constraint simplification, with proofs of semantic preservation.

Result: - Algorithms that compute a normal form for ODRL policies and simplify constraints.
- Proof that normalization preserves policy semantics.
- Size analysis: normal-form size is exponential in the number of attributes and linear in the number of distinct values per attribute.
- Demonstration that normalized policies can be expressed in simpler ODRL fragments and that policy comparison reduces to checking rule identity.

Conclusion: Normalization makes ODRL policies easier to process, interoperate, and compare by collapsing diverse encodings into a standard permission-only form, at the cost of potential exponential growth tied to attribute dimensionality.

Abstract: The ODRL language has become the standard for representing policies and regulations for digital rights. However its complexity is a barrier to its usage, which has caused many related theoretical and practical works to focus on different, and not interoperable, fragments of ODRL. Moreover, semantically equivalent policies can be expressed in numerous different ways, which makes comparing them and processing them harder. Building on top of a recently defined semantics, we tackle these problems by proposing an approach that involves a parametrised normalisation of ODRL policies into its minimal components which reformulates policies with permissions and prohibitions into policies with permissions exclusively, and simplifies complex logic constraints into simple ones. We provide algorithms to compute a normal form for ODRL policies and simplifying numerical and symbolic constraints. We prove that these algorithms preserve the semantics of policies, and analyse the size complexity of the result, which is exponential on the number of attributes and linear on the number of unique values for these attributes. We show how this makes complex policies representable in more basic fragments of ODRL, and how it reduces the problem of policy comparison to the simpler problem of checking if two rules are identical.

Pheromone patterns provide structured, traceable routing evidence.

Existing routing strategies typically rely on expensive LLM-based selectors or static policies, and offer limited controllability for semantic-aware routing under dynamic loads and mixed intents, often resulting in unstable performance and inefficient resource utilization. To address these limitations, we propose AMRO-S, an efficient and interpretable routing framework for Multi-Agent Systems (MAS). AMRO-S models MAS routing as a semantic-conditioned path selection problem, enhancing routing performance through three key mechanisms: First, it leverages a supervised fine-tuned (SFT) small language model for intent inference, providing a low-overhead semantic interface for each query; second, it decomposes routing memory into task-specific pheromone specialists, reducing cross-task interference and optimizing path selection under mixed workloads; finally, it employs a quality-gated asynchronous update mechanism to decouple inference from learning, optimizing routing without increasing latency.
