### [166] [Structured Distillation for Personalized Agent Memory: 11x Token Reduction with Retrieval Preservation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13017)
*Sydney Lewis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a structured distillation layer for single-user AI chat histories that compresses each exchange ~11x (371→38 tokens) while preserving retrieval quality close to verbatim; best distilled hits 96% of verbatim MRR and a cross-layer setup slightly surpasses verbatim. Open-source implementation provided.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long AI conversations are expensive to carry verbatim, yet prior turns contain valuable, personalized context. The goal is to enable affordable, high-recall retrieval over a user’s own chat history without paying full context costs.

Method: Each exchange is compressed into a 4-field object: (1) exchange_core, (2) specific_context, (3) thematic room_assignments, and (4) regex-extracted files_touched. The distilled text averages 38 tokens/exchange. They evaluate on 4,182 conversations (14,340 exchanges) from 6 software-engineering projects using 201 recall queries, 107 search configurations spanning 5 pure and 5 cross-layer modes, and 5 LLM graders, yielding 214,519 consensus-graded query–result pairs. They compare distilled vs verbatim with MRR and apply Bonferroni correction across mechanisms (vector vs BM25).

Result: Compression: 11x (371→38 tokens/exchange). Retrieval: best pure distilled MRR 0.717 (96% of best verbatim 0.745). Mechanism effects: all 20 vector-search configs show no significant degradation after Bonferroni; all 20 BM25 configs degrade significantly (|d|=0.031–0.756). Best cross-layer configuration slightly exceeds the best pure verbatim baseline (MRR 0.759).

Conclusion: Structured distillation can compress single-user agent memory dramatically without uniformly harming retrieval; vector-based retrieval preserves quality best, while BM25 suffers. At 1/11 context cost, thousands of exchanges fit in a single prompt and the verbatim log can be used for drill-down. Code and analysis are released as open source.

Abstract: Long conversations with an AI agent create a simple problem for one user: the history is useful, but carrying it verbatim is expensive. We study personalized agent memory: one user's conversation history with an agent, distilled into a compact retrieval layer for later search. Each exchange is compressed into a compound object with four fields (exchange_core, specific_context, thematic room_assignments, and regex-extracted files_touched). The searchable distilled text averages 38 tokens per exchange. Applied to 4,182 conversations (14,340 exchanges) from 6 software engineering projects, the method reduces average exchange length from 371 to 38 tokens, yielding 11x compression. We evaluate whether personalized recall survives that compression using 201 recall-oriented queries, 107 configurations spanning 5 pure and 5 cross-layer search modes, and 5 LLM graders (214,519 consensus-graded query-result pairs). The best pure distilled configuration reaches 96% of the best verbatim MRR (0.717 vs 0.745). Results are mechanism-dependent. All 20 vector search configurations remain non-significant after Bonferroni correction, while all 20 BM25 configurations degrade significantly (effect sizes |d|=0.031-0.756). The best cross-layer setup slightly exceeds the best pure verbatim baseline (MRR 0.759). Structured distillation compresses single-user agent memory without uniformly sacrificing retrieval quality. At 1/11 the context cost, thousands of exchanges fit within a single prompt while the verbatim source remains available for drill-down. We release the implementation and analysis pipeline as open-source software.

</details>


### [167] [Beyond Final Answers: CRYSTAL Benchmark for Transparent Multimodal Reasoning Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13099)
*Wayner Barrios,SouYoung Jin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CRYSTAL is a multimodal benchmark that scores verifiable reasoning steps (and their order) and shows current MLLMs often cherry‑pick steps and misorder chains; a new multiplicative Causal Process Reward with a curriculum substantially improves step alignment (+32% Match F1) without manual step labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Accuracy on final answers hides whether models reason correctly, traceably, and in order. The field lacks a reliable, step‑level, order‑aware evaluation for multimodal reasoning and a training signal that jointly rewards correct outcomes and faithful intermediate processes without expensive human step annotations.

Method: Construct CRYSTAL with 6,372 multimodal instances and reference trajectories via a Delphi‑style ensemble of four independent MLLMs, semantic clustering, and human quality gates. Define two metrics: Match F1 (semantic precision/recall over steps) and Ordered Match F1 (adds penalties for out‑of‑order steps). Evaluate 20 MLLMs, including frontier commercial models. Propose Causal Process Reward (CPR), a multiplicative reward coupling answer correctness with step‑level alignment, and a CPR‑Curriculum that ramps reasoning difficulty during GRPO training; compare against additive rewards.

Result: Evaluation reveals systematic issues invisible to accuracy: widespread cherry‑picking (precision ≫ recall), non‑monotonic scaling trade‑offs, and disordered chains—no competitive model preserves >60% of matched steps in the correct order. Training with CPR‑Curriculum yields a +32% Match F1 gain under GRPO, where additive strategies fail, improving reasoning quality without manual step annotations.

Conclusion: Process‑level, order‑aware evaluation surfaces hidden weaknesses in current MLLMs. CRYSTAL plus CPR offers a practical path to align models’ reasoning steps with correct answers and to train better chain‑of‑thought without step labels; future progress should target process fidelity, not just accuracy.

Abstract: We introduce **CRYSTAL** (*__C__lear __R__easoning via __Y__ielded __S__teps, __T__raceability and __L__ogic*), a diagnostic benchmark with 6,372 instances that evaluates multimodal reasoning through verifiable intermediate steps. We propose two complementary metrics: *Match F1*, which scores step-level precision and recall via semantic similarity matching, and *Ordered Match F1*, which further penalizes disordered reasoning chains. References are constructed through a Delphi-inspired pipeline where four independent MLLMs generate trajectories, aggregated via semantic clustering and validated through human quality gates. Evaluation of 20 MLLMs, including commercial frontier systems not used during benchmark construction, reveals systematic failures invisible to accuracy: universal cherry-picking (precision far exceeds recall), non-monotonic scaling trade-offs, and disordered reasoning where no competitive model preserves more than 60% of matched steps in correct order. Beyond evaluation, we propose the **Causal Process Reward (CPR)**, a multiplicative reward that couples answer correctness with step-level alignment, and **CPR-Curriculum**, which progressively increases reasoning difficulty during training. CPR-Curriculum achieves +32% Match F1 via GRPO where additive reward strategies fail, improving reasoning without manual step annotation.

</details>


### [168] [Steve-Evolving: Open-World Embodied Self-Evolution via Fine-Grained Diagnosis and Dual-Track Knowledge Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13131)
*Zhengwei Xie,Zhisheng Chen,Ziyan Weng,Tingyu Wu,Chenglong Li,Vireo Zhang,Kun Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Steve-Evolving is a non-parametric, self-evolving framework that organizes fine-grained interaction experiences, distills successes into reusable skills and failures into guardrails, and injects both into an LLM planner for closed-loop, retraining-free improvement on long-horizon embodied tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon, open-world tasks challenge embodied agents not because of single-step planning, but due to how interaction experiences are organized, recalled, and evolved over time. Static memory/retrieval and binary success signals are too coarse to attribute errors or generalize successes. There is a need for auditable, information-dense experience structures that can continually refine behavior without updating model parameters.

Method: The framework has three phases. (1) Experience Anchoring logs each subgoal attempt as (pre-state, action, diagnosis-result, post-state) and stores it in a three-tier experience space indexed by condition signatures, spatial hashes, semantic tags, with rolling summaries for efficient recall. The execution layer emits rich diagnosis signals: state-difference summaries, enumerated failure causes, continuous indicators, and stagnation/loop detection. (2) Experience Distillation converts successful trajectories into reusable skills with explicit preconditions and verification criteria, and turns failures into executable guardrails capturing root causes and forbidding risky operations at subgoal/task levels. (3) Knowledge-Driven Closed-Loop Control retrieves skills and guardrails, injects them into an LLM planner, and performs diagnosis-triggered local replanning that updates constraints online—achieving continual evolution without parameter updates.

Result: On the Minecraft MCU long-horizon benchmark, Steve-Evolving consistently outperforms static-retrieval baselines, indicating better success and robustness, though exact metrics are not specified in the abstract.

Conclusion: Coupling fine-grained diagnosis with dual-track distillation (skills and guardrails) in a closed loop enables non-parametric continual improvement. This design yields stronger long-horizon control and safer behavior without retraining, supported by empirical gains over static retrieval approaches.

Abstract: Open-world embodied agents must solve long-horizon tasks where the main bottleneck is not single-step planning quality but how interaction experience is organized and evolved. To this end, we present Steve-Evolving, a non-parametric self-evolving framework that tightly couples fine-grained execution diagnosis with dual-track knowledge distillation in a closed loop. The method follows three phases: Experience Anchoring, Experience Distillation, and Knowledge-Driven Closed-Loop Control. In detail, Experience Anchoring solidifies each subgoal attempt into a structured experience tuple with a fixed schema (pre-state, action, diagnosis-result, and post-state) and organizes it in a three-tier experience space with multi-dimensional indices (e.g., condition signatures, spatial hashing, and semantic tags) plus rolling summarization for efficient and auditable recall. To ensure sufficient information density for attribution, the execution layer provides compositional diagnosis signals beyond binary outcomes, including state-difference summaries, enumerated failure causes, continuous indicators, and stagnation/loop detection. Moreover, successful trajectories of Experience Distillation are generalized into reusable skills with explicit preconditions and verification criteria, while failures are distilled into executable guardrails that capture root causes and forbid risky operations at both subgoal and task granularities. Besides, Knowledge-Driven Closed-Loop Control retrieved skills and guardrails are injected into an LLM planner, and diagnosis-triggered local replanning updates the active constraints online, forming a continual evolution process without any model parameter updates. Experiments on the long-horizon suite of Minecraft MCU demonstrate consistent improvements over static-retrieval baselines.

</details>


### [169] [When Right Meets Wrong: Bilateral Context Conditioning with Reward-Confidence Correction for GRPO](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13134)
*Yu Li,Tian Lan,Zhengling Qi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They reinterpret GRPO through a contrastive lens and add two plug-in mechanisms—BiCC for cross-sample conditioning between correct and incorrect traces, and RCC for variance-stabilized advantages—yielding consistent gains on math reasoning without extra sampling or auxiliary models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard GRPO computes group-relative advantages but optimizes each output independently, failing to exploit the natural within-group contrast between correct and incorrect reasoning traces and suffering from instability due to variance in advantages. The authors aim to leverage this comparative signal and stabilize training.

Method: 1) Contrastive reformulation: show GRPO implicitly maximizes the margin between policy ratios of correct vs. incorrect samples. 2) Bilateral Context Conditioning (BiCC): during optimization, allow each sample to reference paired successful and failed traces, enabling direct cross-sample information flow. 3) Reward-Confidence Correction (RCC): dynamically adjust the GRPO advantage baseline using reward–confidence covariance from a first-order approximation to the variance-minimizing estimator. Both are plug-in, sampling-free, and compatible with GRPO variants.

Result: On mathematical reasoning benchmarks, the proposed methods produce consistent performance improvements across multiple models and GRPO-style algorithms (no specific numbers provided in the abstract).

Conclusion: Viewing GRPO contrastively clarifies its objective as margin maximization. BiCC and RCC exploit within-group contrasts and stabilize learning, serving as general, lightweight extensions that improve reasoning model training without added sampling or auxiliary networks.

Abstract: Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) has emerged as an effective method for training reasoning models. While it computes advantages based on group mean, GRPO treats each output as an independent sample during the optimization and overlooks a vital structural signal: the natural contrast between correct and incorrect solutions within the same group, thus ignoring the rich, comparative data that could be leveraged by explicitly pitting successful reasoning traces against failed ones. To capitalize on this, we present a contrastive reformulation of GRPO, showing that the GRPO objective implicitly maximizes the margin between the policy ratios of correct and incorrect samples. Building on this insight, we propose Bilateral Context Conditioning (BICC), a mechanism that allows the model to cross-reference successful and failed reasoning traces during the optimization, enabling a direct information flow across samples. We further introduce Reward-Confidence Correction (RCC) to stabilize training by dynamically adjusts the advantage baseline in GRPO using reward-confidence covariance derived from the first-order approximation of the variance-minimizing estimator. Both mechanisms require no additional sampling or auxiliary models and can be adapted to all GRPO variants. Experiments on mathematical reasoning benchmarks demonstrate consistent improvements across comprehensive models and algorithms. Code is available at \href{https://github.com/Skylanding/BiCC}{https://github.com/Skylanding/BiCC}.

</details>


### [170] [Developing and evaluating a chatbot to support maternal health care](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13168)
*Smriti Jha,Vidhi Jain,Jianyu Xu,Grace Liu,Sowmya Ramesh,Jitender Nagpal,Gretchen Chapman,Benjamin Bellows,Siddhartha Goyal,Aarti Singh,Bryan Wilder*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Defense-in-depth maternal health chatbot for India that combines stage-aware triage, curated-guideline retrieval, and evidence-conditioned LLM generation, paired with a multi-pronged evaluation; achieves high emergency recall and shows no single model/evaluation suffices for safety in multilingual, noisy settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Provide trustworthy, context-aware maternal health information via phone chatbots in low-resource Indian settings where users have low health literacy, code-mixed queries, and limited access to care; ensure safe routing and grounding despite short, underspecified inputs and missing symptom context.

Method: Built a system with (1) stage-aware triage that routes high-risk queries to expert-crafted templates, (2) hybrid retrieval over curated maternal/newborn care guidelines, and (3) evidence-conditioned generation from an LLM. Core contribution is an evaluation workflow under limited expert supervision: (i) labeled triage benchmark (N=150) with explicit missed-emergency vs. over-escalation trade-off, achieving 86.7% emergency recall; (ii) synthetic multi-evidence retrieval benchmark (N=100) with chunk-level labels; (iii) LLM-as-judge comparisons on real queries (N=781) using clinician co-designed criteria; and (iv) expert validation.

Result: Triage component reaches 86.7% emergency recall on N=150; created a synthetic retrieval benchmark (N=100) with fine-grained evidence labels; conducted LLM-as-judge evaluation on N=781 real queries with clinician-designed rubrics; expert review validates approach. Findings indicate layered architecture and multi-method evaluation outperform reliance on any single model or metric for trustworthy performance in noisy, multilingual contexts.

Conclusion: Trustworthy maternal health assistants in multilingual, low-resource environments require a defense-in-depth architecture (triage + curated retrieval + evidence-conditioned generation) and comprehensive, multi-method evaluation. Reporting safety trade-offs and incorporating clinician input are essential; no single model or evaluation method is sufficient for safe deployment.

Abstract: The ability to provide trustworthy maternal health information using phone-based chatbots can have a significant impact, particularly in low-resource settings where users have low health literacy and limited access to care. However, deploying such systems is technically challenging: user queries are short, underspecified, and code-mixed across languages, answers require regional context-specific grounding, and partial or missing symptom context makes safe routing decisions difficult.
  We present a chatbot for maternal health in India developed through a partnership between academic researchers, a health tech company, a public health nonprofit, and a hospital. The system combines (1) stage-aware triage, routing high-risk queries to expert templates, (2) hybrid retrieval over curated maternal/newborn guidelines, and (3) evidence-conditioned generation from an LLM. Our core contribution is an evaluation workflow for high-stakes deployment under limited expert supervision. Targeting both component-level and end-to-end testing, we introduce: (i) a labeled triage benchmark (N=150) achieving 86.7% emergency recall, explicitly reporting the missed-emergency vs. over-escalation trade-off; (ii) a synthetic multi-evidence retrieval benchmark (N=100) with chunk-level evidence labels; (iii) LLM-as-judge comparison on real queries (N=781) using clinician-codesigned criteria; and (iv) expert validation. Our findings show that trustworthy medical assistants in multilingual, noisy settings require defense-in-depth design paired with multi-method evaluation, rather than any single model and evaluation method choice.

### [171] [Semantic Invariance in Agentic AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13173)
*I. de Zarzà,J. de Curtò,Jordi Cabot,Pietro Manzoni,Carlos T. Calafate*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces a metamorphic testing framework to evaluate whether LLM agents’ reasoning remains stable under semantically equivalent input variations (“semantic invariance”). Across 7 models and 19 multi-step scientific problems, robustness does not scale with size; Qwen3-30B-A3B is most stable (79.6% invariance, 0.91 semantic similarity).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard accuracy benchmarks on fixed prompts miss a critical reliability dimension: whether an LLM agent gives consistent reasoning and answers when the same problem is posed in semantically equivalent ways. Safety- and decision-critical deployments require such stability guarantees.

Method: Define and apply eight semantic-preserving input transformations (identity, paraphrase, fact reordering, expansion, contraction, academic context, business context, contrastive formulation) to 19 multi-step reasoning tasks across eight scientific domains. Evaluate seven LLMs from four families (Hermes 70B/405B, Qwen3 30B-A3B/235B-A22B, DeepSeek-R1, gpt-oss 20B/120B). Measure invariance via rate of identical or equivalent outputs and semantic similarity between responses.

Result: Model scale is not a predictor of robustness. Qwen3-30B-A3B achieves the highest stability (79.6% invariant responses; average semantic similarity 0.91), while larger models are more brittle under the same transformations.

Conclusion: Semantic invariance is a distinct reliability criterion that current benchmarks overlook. Metamorphic testing exposes fragility in larger models and suggests that smaller, well-aligned models can be more stable. Robust deployment should incorporate invariance testing and training/tuning strategies aimed at invariance rather than relying on scale alone.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) increasingly serve as autonomous reasoning agents in decision support, scientific problem-solving, and multi-agent coordination systems. However, deploying LLM agents in consequential applications requires assurance that their reasoning remains stable under semantically equivalent input variations, a property we term semantic invariance.Standard benchmark evaluations, which assess accuracy on fixed, canonical problem formulations, fail to capture this critical reliability dimension. To address this shortcoming, in this paper we present a metamorphic testing framework for systematically assessing the robustness of LLM reasoning agents, applying eight semantic-preserving transformations (identity, paraphrase, fact reordering, expansion, contraction, academic context, business context, and contrastive formulation) across seven foundation models spanning four distinct architectural families: Hermes (70B, 405B), Qwen3 (30B-A3B, 235B-A22B), DeepSeek-R1, and gpt-oss (20B, 120B). Our evaluation encompasses 19 multi-step reasoning problems across eight scientific domains. The results reveal that model scale does not predict robustness: the smaller Qwen3-30B-A3B achieves the highest stability (79.6% invariant responses, semantic similarity 0.91), while larger models exhibit greater fragility.

</details>
